--- PROMPT ---

# JSON-Structured Prompt for Calculus Questions

**Critical Rule:** *Solve and verify all mathematics **before** writing the question.* If you discover an error while solving, **restart that problem with different numbers**. After three failed attempts, abandon the problem and start a new one.

**Target Topic(s):** Continuity
**Target Difficulty:** D2 (Medium) - Requires analysis or multi-step reasoning, 3-5 steps
**Number of Questions:** 1

---
## Reference Examples (JSON format)

Study these examples carefully. Pay special attention to:
- How the `distractors` field shows WHY each wrong answer is tempting
- The `common_errors` that students make
- The relationship between `difficulty` and `cognitive_level`
- The precision and accuracy of the explanations

### Example 1
```json
{
  "text": "Let $f$ be the function defined by $f(x) = |x - 2|$ for all $x$. Which of the following statements is true?",
  "answers": [
    {"text": "$f$ is continuous but not differentiable at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": true},
    {"text": "$f$ is differentiable at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f$ is not continuous at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$\\displaystyle\\lim_{x \\to 2} f(x) \\ne 0$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$x = 2$ is a vertical asymptote of the graph of $f$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "The absolute value function has a corner at $x = 2$. It is continuous but not differentiable at that point because the left and right derivatives differ.",
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

### Example 2
```json
{
  "text": "*[Graph: Function $f$ on $[-2, 2]$ with a cusp at $x = 0$.]*The function $f$, whose graph is shown above, is defined on $-2 \\le x \\le 2$. Which of the following statements about $f$ is false?",
  "answers": [
    {"text": "$f$ is continuous at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f$ is differentiable at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": true},
    {"text": "$f$ has a critical point at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f$ has an absolute minimum at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "The concavity of the graph of $f$ changes at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "A function with a cusp is continuous but not differentiable at that point. Statement B is FALSE.",
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

### Example 3
```json
{
  "text": "If the function $f$ is continuous at $x = 3$, which of the following must be true?",
  "answers": [
    {"text": "$f(3) < \\lim_{x \\to 3} f(x)$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$\\lim_{x \\to 3^-} f(x) \\ne \\lim_{x \\to 3^+} f(x)$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f(3) = \\lim_{x \\to 3^-} f(x) = \\lim_{x \\to 3^+} f(x)$", "is_correct": true},
    {"text": "The derivative of $f$ at $x = 3$ exists.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "The derivative of $f$ is positive for $x < 3$ and negative for $x > 3$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "By definition of continuity: $\\lim_{x \\to 3} f(x) = f(3)$, which requires both one-sided limits to equal $f(3)$.",
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

---

## Workflow (follow in order)
1. **Plan & Solve**
   - Decide on the exact concept and solve it completely on scratch paper.
   - Produce a step‑by‑step solution with all calculus/algebra shown, including checks.
2. **Design Distractors**
   - Review your solution and identify TWO mistakes a student could make
   - For each mistake, quickly rework the problem and determine the answer resulting from that mistake
   - Produce one new answer choice for each of the two mistakes.
   - Now look at the correct answer and produce two more wrong answers from simple algebraic or numerical transformations (such as making it negative, off-by-one, etc)
   - You will end up with one correct and four wrong answer choices.
3. **Write Output**
  - Return a **single JSON array** of question objects.
  - Output JSON only. Do not include any text before or after the array.
   - **Formatting Rules**
     * Use ONLY `$...$` for inline math and `$$...$$` for display math.
     * No HTML tags.

*** User instructions


**Output Format (JSON Array):**

Return your response as a JSON array containing 1 question object(s). Each question should follow this structure:

```json
[
  {
    "text": "Question text here with markdown formatting like **bold** or `code`. Can include code blocks:\n\n```java\nSystem.out.println(\"Hello\");\n```",
    "explanation": "Step-by-step walkthrough of how to arrive at the correct answer. Verify correct answer. Design distractors.",
    "distractors": "Analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and what misconception leads to it",
    "answers": [
      {"text": "Answer text (use markdown like `42` for code)", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Why this is wrong"},
      {"text": "Another answer", "is_correct": true, "explanation": "Why this is correct"},
      {"text": "Third answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Common misconception"},
      {"text": "Fourth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Off-by-one error"},
      {"text": "Fifth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Calculation mistake"}
    ]
  }
]
```
*** User instructions

Without specifying c, we can select k to make numerator zero at 3, then Q(3) becomes whatever.

We have N(x) = 3x^2 - kx -12. Suppose N(x) = (x-3)(Ax + B). Expand: (x-3)(Ax + B) = A x^2 + B x -3A x -3B = A x^2 + (B -3A)x -3B. Compare coefficients: A = 3. Then coefficient of x: B - 3A = B -9 = -k => B = -k +9. Constant term: -3B = -12 => B=4. Then B=4 gives -k+9=4 => -k = -5 => k=5. Good. Then Q(x) = 3x + 4. So limit = Q(3)=13. Therefore to be continuous at x=3 we need f(3)=13 not 5. Since problem gives f(3)=5, cannot be continuous for any k? Let's check maybe we can also adjust c (value at point) rather than k. --- RESPONSE ---

We need to follow the workflow: first pick a continuity question of medium difficulty requiring 3-5 steps. Solve then create answer choices and distractors, explanation.

Important: Must solve and verify mathematics before writing the question. If any error discovered, restart with different numbers (up to three attempts). I need to ensure the math is correct.

Let's design a question: maybe about continuity of a piecewise function at a point. Provide function definition, ask which statement is true about continuity at a point or find a parameter that makes function continuous.

E.g., function:

\(f(x) = \begin{cases}
ax^2 + 3x + 1, & x < 2 \\
bx + 5, & x \ge 2
\end{cases}\)

Find values of a and b that make f continuous at x=2 and also maybe at another point.

But we need multiple-choice with only one correct answer. Could ask: For what value of a does the function become continuous? Provide options.

Proceed: Let's design function with a parameter a only, making it easier.

\(f(x) = \begin{cases}
x^2 + 2x - 3, & x \le 1 \\
ax + 4, & x > 1
\end{cases}\)

Find a that makes f continuous at x=1.

Compute left limit: f(1) = 1^2 + 2*1 -3 = 1 +2 -3 =0. So left limit =0 = f(1). Right limit as x→1+ is a*1+4 = a+4. For continuity need a+4 =0 => a = -4.

So correct answer a = -4.

Now produce distractors: common mistake: compute left limit incorrectly (maybe get -2). Let's compute left limit incorrectly: 1^2 + 2*1 -3 =0 correct; maybe student forget -3 and get 3? Actually they'd get 1+2 =3, forgetting -3 gives 3. Then set a+4 =3 → a = -1. That's a plausible distractor.

Another mistake: using f(1) from right piece incorrectly (since piece defined for x>1, but student might think it's equal at x=1). They could set continuity condition as left limit = right piece at x=1, i.e., a*1+4 =0 => a = -4 (same as correct). Not a distractor.

Better distractor: solving for a using derivative continuity (common error). They might think need equal slopes: derivative left = a. Left derivative for x^2+2x-3 is 2x+2, at x=1 gives 2*1+2=4. Set a=4 (incorrect). So a=4.

Another distractor: compute left limit as -2 (maybe mis-evaluate x^2 + 2x -3 at 1 incorrectly as -2?). Let's see: If they think 1^2+2*1 =3, minus 3 => 0, that's correct. So maybe they mis-evaluate as (1)^2 =1, 2*1 =2, 1+2 =3, minus 3 =0. Not error.

Maybe they think continuity condition is a+4 = something else like maybe they think need to evaluate at x=0? Not.

Better: Provide distractor using off-by-one: think continuity at x=0 and set a+4 = something else. But the function changes at x=1, not 0.

Simplify: Provide options: a = -4 (correct), a = -1 (from forgetting -3), a = 4 (using derivative continuity), a = 0 (thinking any a works), a = 2 (random). That's five options.

Now we need explanation, distractors analysis.

Now we need to verify math: left f(1) =0; right limit = a+4. So a = -4 for continuity. Good.

Now produce JSON array with one object.

Now fill fields: "text": include description and ask: Which value of a makes f continuous at x=1?

"answers": list five choices each with text, is_correct, explanation.

Add "explanation": step-by-step.

Add "distractors": analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting.

Ok.

Make sure to use $...$ for math.

    "explanation": "To ensure continuity at $x=1$ we need the left‑hand limit, the right‑hand limit, and the function value at $x=1$ to be equal.\n\n1. **Left‑hand value** (the function is defined by $x^{2}+2x-3$ for $x\\le 1$):\n   $$f(1)=1^{2}+2\\cdot1-3=1+2-3=0.$$\n2. **Right‑hand limit** (use the expression $ax+4$ as $x\\to1^{+}$):\n   $$\\lim_{x\\to1^{+}}f(x)=a\\cdot1+4=a+4.$$ \n3. **Continuity condition**:\n   $$f(1)=\\lim_{x\\to1^{-}}f(x)=\\lim_{x\\to1^{+}}f(x) \\quad\\Longrightarrow\\quad 0=a+4.$$\n4. # JSON-Structured Prompt for Calculus Questions

**Critical Rule:** *Solve and verify all mathematics **before** writing the question.* If you discover an error while solving, **restart that problem with different numbers**. After three failed attempts, abandon the problem and start a new one.

**Target Topic(s):** Continuity
**Target Difficulty:** D2 (Medium) - Requires analysis or multi-step reasoning, 3-5 steps
**Number of Questions:** 4

---
## Reference Examples (JSON format)

Study these examples carefully. Pay special attention to:
- How the `distractors` field shows WHY each wrong answer is tempting
- The `common_errors` that students make
- The relationship between `difficulty` and `cognitive_level`
- The precision and accuracy of the explanations

### Example 1
```json
{
  "text": "Let $f$ be the function defined by $f(x) = |x - 2|$ for all $x$. Which of the following statements is true?",
  "answers": [
    {"text": "$f$ is continuous but not differentiable at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": true},
    {"text": "$f$ is differentiable at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f$ is not continuous at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$\\displaystyle\\lim_{x \\to 2} f(x) \\ne 0$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$x = 2$ is a vertical asymptote of the graph of $f$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "The absolute value function has a corner at $x = 2$. It is continuous but not differentiable at that point because the left and right derivatives differ.",
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

### Example 2
```json
{
  "text": "*[Graph: Function $f$ on $[-2, 2]$ with a cusp at $x = 0$.]*The function $f$, whose graph is shown above, is defined on $-2 \\le x \\le 2$. Which of the following statements about $f$ is false?",
  "answers": [
    {"text": "$f$ is continuous at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f$ is differentiable at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": true},
    {"text": "$f$ has a critical point at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f$ has an absolute minimum at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "The concavity of the graph of $f$ changes at $x = 0$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "A function with a cusp is continuous but not differentiable at that point. Statement B is FALSE.",
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

### Example 3
```json
{
  "text": "If the function $f$ is continuous at $x = 3$, which of the following must be true?",
  "answers": [
    {"text": "$f(3) < \\lim_{x \\to 3} f(x)$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$\\lim_{x \\to 3^-} f(x) \\ne \\lim_{x \\to 3^+} f(x)$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f(3) = \\lim_{x \\to 3^-} f(x) = \\lim_{x \\to 3^+} f(x)$", "is_correct": true},
    {"text": "The derivative of $f$ at $x = 3$ exists.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "The derivative of $f$ is positive for $x < 3$ and negative for $x > 3$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "By definition of continuity: $\\lim_{x \\to 3} f(x) = f(3)$, which requires both one-sided limits to equal $f(3)$.",
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

---

## Workflow (follow in order)
1. **Plan & Solve**
   - Decide on the exact concept and solve it completely on scratch paper.
   - Produce a step‑by‑step solution with all calculus/algebra shown, including checks.
2. **Design Distractors**
   - Review your solution and identify TWO mistakes a student could make
   - For each mistake, quickly rework the problem and determine the answer resulting from that mistake
   - Produce one new answer choice for each of the two mistakes.
   - Now look at the correct answer and produce two more wrong answers from simple algebraic or numerical transformations (such as making it negative, off-by-one, etc)
   - You will end up with one correct and four wrong answer choices.
3. **Write Output**
  - Return a **single JSON array** of question objects.
  - Output JSON only. Do not include any text before or after the array.
   - **Formatting Rules**
     * Use ONLY `$...$` for inline math and `$$...$$` for display math.
     * No HTML tags.

*** User instructions


**Output Format (JSON Array):**

Return your response as a JSON array containing 4 question object(s). Each question should follow this structure:

```json
[
  {
    "text": "Question text here with markdown formatting like **bold** or `code`. Can include code blocks:\n\n```java\nSystem.out.println(\"Hello\");\n```",
    "explanation": "Step-by-step walkthrough of how to arrive at the correct answer. Verify correct answer. Design distractors.",
    "distractors": "Analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and what misconception leads to it",
    "answers": [
      {"text": "Answer text (use markdown like `42` for code)", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Why this is wrong"},
      {"text": "Another answer", "is_correct": true, "explanation": "Why this is correct"},
      {"text": "Third answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Common misconception"},
      {"text": "Fourth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Off-by-one error"},
      {"text": "Fifth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Calculation mistake"}
    ]
  }
]
```
*** User instructions


--- RESPONSE ---

We need to produce 4 questions on continuity, medium difficulty, multi-step reasoning. Must follow critical rule: solve and verify all mathematics before writing the question. If error, restart. Must produce json array with 4 question objects. Each object has fields: text, explanation, distractors, answers (list of 5 answer objects each with text, is_correct, explanation). We need to design distractors analysis: explanation field summarises solution; distractors field analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting.

Need to ensure correct answer is indeed correct after verification.

Let's think of four continuity problems that involve piecewise functions, limit equality, removable discontinuities, intermediate value theorem (continuity implication), and perhaps continuity of a rational function after simplification.

We need 5 answer choices: one correct, four wrong. Need two mistakes-based distractors plus two other wrongs.

Let's craft each problem fully.

We'll solve each.

Problem 1: Piecewise function with parameter; find which x values where continuous.

Define f(x)= { (x^2-4)/(x-2) for x≠2, 5 for x=2 }. Determine continuity at x=2.

Compute limit as x→2 of (x^2-4)/(x-2) = (x-2)(x+2)/(x-2) = x+2, limit = 4. Since f(2)=5, not equal => discontinuous. So answer: f is not continuous at x=2.

But we need multi-choice about continuity maybe "f is continuous for all real numbers" vs "continuous everywhere except x=2" vs "continuous only at x=2" etc.

Let's design question: "Which of the following statements about the function f is true?" Provide options.

Correct: "f is continuous for all x ≠ 2 but discontinuous at x = 2."

Other distractors: 1) "f is continuous everywhere." (mistake ignoring point value) 2) "f is discontinuous at x = 2 only because denominator zero" (but they may think domain issue). But we need proper answer text.

Ok.

Problem 2: Determine limit and continuity for a piecewise function with absolute value.

Define g(x)= { x^2 for x ≤ 1, 2x-1 for x > 1 }. Ask: Is g continuous at x=1? Compute left limit: 1^2 =1. Right limit: 2*1-1=1. Since both =1, and g(1) = 1^2 =1. So continuous.

Now ask which statement is true.

Correct: "g is continuous at x = 1."

Distractors: "g has a jump discontinuity at x = 1." (wrong). "g is not defined at x = 1." (wrong). "The limit as x→1 does not exist." (wrong). Another maybe "g is differentiable at x=1." (might be false? Let's check derivative from left =2x at x=1 =>2. From right derivative =2. So actually differentiable! So that would be correct statement, but we need false statements. We can give "g is not differentiable at x=1." That's a wrong if they think cusp.

We'll need only one correct; others wrong.

Problem 3: Use Intermediate Value Theorem: f(x)= x^3 - 3x + 1 on interval [0,2]. Determine if there exists c where f(c)=0. Compute f(0)=1, f(2)=8-6+1=3. Both positive, so cannot guarantee root. But actually f(x) might cross zero? Let's compute f(1)=1-3+1=-1. So there is root between 0 and2 because sign change between 0 and1 actually. But we need continuity: polynomial continuous. So there is c in (0,1) such that f(c)=0.

Question: Which of the following statements is guaranteed by continuity? Provide statements.

Correct: "There exists at least one number c in (0,1) such that f(c)=0."

Distractors: "There exists a number c in (1,2) such that f(c)=0." (no sign change). "f has a maximum at x=0." (not guaranteed). "f is decreasing on [0,2]." (false). "f has exactly one root in [0,2]." (not guaranteed; could be more). Good.

Problem 4: Determine continuity of rational function after cancellation: h(x) = (x^2 - 9)/(x-3). Domain x≠3. Simplify to x+3 for x≠3. Define piecewise: h(x)=x+3 for x≠3, and h(3)=6. Is h continuous at x=3? Evaluate limit as x→3 of x+3 =6, value =6, so continuous. So statement: "h is continuous for all real numbers."

But need to include subtlety: The original expression is undefined at 3, but with definition at 3 it's continuous. So options: "h is continuous everywhere." "h has a removable discontinuity at x=3." "h is discontinuous at x=3 because denominator zero." "h is continuous for all x≠3 but discontinuous at x=3." "h is not defined at x=3."

Correct answer: "h is continuous for all real numbers."

Now create answer objects: must include explanation per answer.

Also need distractors field with analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and misconception.

Structure demands "answers": list of objects each with text, is_correct, explanation. So need per answer explanation.

Also overall explanation field summarizing solution.

Let's craft each.

We need to ensure we follow JSON format with proper escaping of backslashes maybe not needed.

Let's produce JSON array with four objects.

**Number of Questions:** 1

```json
    {"text": "$f$ is differentiable at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": false},
```

## Workflow (follow in order)
1. **Plan & Solve**
   - Decide on the exact concept and solve it completely on scratch paper.
   - Produce a step‑by‑step solution with all calculus/algebra shown, including checks.
2. **Design Distractors**
   - Review your solution and identify TWO mistakes a student could make
   - For each mistake, quickly rework the problem and determine the answer resulting from that mistake
   - Produce one new answer choice for each of the two mistakes.
   - Now look at the correct answer and produce two more wrong answers from simple algebraic or numerical transformations (such as making it negative, off-by-one, etc)
   - You will end up with one correct and four wrong answer choices.
3. **Write Output**
  - Return a **single JSON array** of question objects.
  - Output JSON only. Do not include any text before or after the array.
   - **Formatting Rules**
     * Use ONLY `$...$` for inline math and `$$...$$` for display math.
     * No HTML tags.

*** User instructions


**Output Format (JSON Array):**

Return your response as a JSON array containing 1 question object(s). Each question should follow this structure:

```json
[
  {
    "text": "Question text here with markdown formatting like **bold** or `code`. Can include code blocks:\n\n```java\nSystem.out.println(\"Hello\");\n```",

      {"text": "Answer text (use markdown like `42` for code)", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Why this is wrong"},
      {"text": "Another answer", "is_correct": true, "explanation": "Why this is correct"},

      {"text": "Fifth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Calculation mistake"}
```

--- RESPONSE ---

We need to produce one question about continuity, medium difficulty, 3-5 steps. Must follow critical rule: solve and verify before writing question; if error, restart with different numbers. Let's craft a problem: perhaps function piecewise with a parameter, ask about continuity at a point.

Example: Let f(x) = { (x^2 - 4)/(x-2) for x ≠ 2; a for x = 2}. Determine value of a that makes f continuous at x=2. That's classic: simplify expression = x+2, limit = 4, so a=4.

That's straightforward maybe too easy. Need medium with multi-step reasoning 3-5 steps. Maybe involve absolute value or square root, requiring domain considerations.

Example: f(x) = { (x^2 - 9)/(x-3) for x < 5; sqrt(x-5) for x ≥ 5 }. Determine whether f is continuous at x=5. Evaluate limit from left: simplify (x^2-9)/(x-3) = x+3, limit as x→5- = 8. Right-hand limit sqrt(5-5)=0, so discontinuous. That's good.

But we also need answer choices: maybe ask which statement is true about continuity at x=5? Provide statements.

Alternatively ask find the value of parameter k such that piecewise function is continuous at x=1 and x=3.

Let's create function:

f(x) = {
   (2x^2 - kx) / (x-1)   for x < 1,
   3x + 2                for 1 ≤ x ≤ 3,
   sqrt(4 - x)           for x > 3
}

Find value of k that makes f continuous at x=1, and determine if f is continuous at x=3.

That's multi-step: limit from left at 1, evaluate expression, simplify; then also check continuity at 3 (right side sqrt). Need also check domain: sqrt(4 - x) defined for x ≤4; at x=3 it's sqrt(1)=1, left value from middle piece at x=3 is 3*3+2=11. So discontinuous at 3 regardless. So answer could be something like: "The function is continuous at x=1 when k = 2, but it is discontinuous at x=3." Provide answer choices with variations.

Let's solve:

For x<1, expression: (2x^2 - kx)/(x-1). As x approaches 1, denominator 0, need numerator also zero for removable discontinuity: 2*1^2 - k*1 = 2 - k = 0 => k = 2. Then we can factor numerator: 2x^2 - 2x = 2x(x-1). Then limit = limit of 2x(x-1)/(x-1) = 2x -> at x=1 gives 2. So left-hand limit =2. Right-hand value at x=1 (since 1≤x) is 3*1+2 =5. Wait this mismatches. Actually since piece changes at x=1 inclusive in middle piece. For continuity at x=1 we need limit from left = value at 1 (the middle piece). So we need limit = 5. Let's compute limit with k=2 gave 2. Not good. So we must solve for k such that limit equals 5.

Compute limit L = limit_{x→1^-} (2x^2 - kx)/(x-1). For limit to exist finite, numerator must also zero at x=1, so 2 - k =0 => k=2. But then limit = 2x →2. Therefore the only way to have limit finite is k=2, but then limit 2 not 5, cannot match value 5. So continuity at x=1 impossible? That could be a point. But maybe we can allow infinite limit? No.

Thus maybe we need to match piecewise differently: maybe middle piece at x=1 is something else variable? Let's adjust middle piece to be 2x (so at x=1 it's 2). Then continuity possible. Let's modify.

Define:

f(x) = {
   (2x^2 - kx) / (x-1)   for x < 1,
   2x                    for 1 ≤ x ≤ 3,
   sqrt(4 - x)           for x > 3
}

Now continuity at x=1: middle value at 1 is 2. Need limit from left = 2, with condition k=2 gives limit =2*1 =2. So k=2 works.

At x=3: left limit from middle piece at 3 is 2*3=6. Right side: sqrt(4-3)=1. So discontinuous at x=3.

Thus answer: k=2, continuous at x=1, but discontinuous at x=3.

Now question: "Which of the following statements is true?" Provide options: A: The function is continuous at both x=1 and x=3. B: The function is continuous at x=1 but not at x=3 (with k=2). C: The function is continuous at x=3 but not at x=1 (any k). D: The function cannot be made continuous at x=1 for any real k. E: The function is continuous nowhere.

We need correct answer B, given k=2.

But need to embed the condition in question: maybe ask "Find the value of k that makes f continuous at x=1. Then, which of the following statements about continuity of f is correct?" Could incorporate both.

Let's craft question text:

"Consider the piecewise function

\[
f(x)=\begin{cases}

\sqrt{4-x}, & x>3.
\end{cases}
\]

Find the real number \(k\) that makes \(f\) continuous at \(x=1\). Then, which of the following statements about the continuity of \(f\) (with that value of \(k\)) is **true**?"

Then answer choices as above.

Now need to design distractors based on common errors: mistake 1: forget to require numerator zero, solving limit incorrectly -> get k any value leading to limit infinite, choose k=0. Mistake 2: compute limit incorrectly using L'Hopital get 2/k maybe. Or mis-evaluate sqrt side at x=3 as sqrt(4-3)=sqrt(1)=1 correct, but some think equals 3. Provide statement that says function is continuous at both points (wrong) maybe from mistake that both limits equal 2; or think k=0 works to make continuity. Provide other wrong statements.

Now we need to produce explanation: step-by-step solving.

Also provide `distractors` field summarizing why each wrong answer is tempting.

We also need `answers` each with `text`, `is_correct`, `explanation`.

Make sure JSON format correct.

Let's craft answer choices:

1. "The function is continuous at both \(x=1\) and \(x=3\)." (False: not continuous at 3) – distractor: from thinking sqrt side matches 6 maybe miscalc.

2. "The function is continuous at \(x=1\) but not at \(x=3\)." (True) – correct.

3. "The function is continuous at \(x=3\) but not at \(x=1\) (for any real \(k\))." (False) – misunderstanding.

4. "No real value of \(k\) can make \(f\) continuous at \(x=1\)." (False) – maybe they think numerator can't be zero etc.

5. "The function is discontinuous everywhere." (False) – extreme.

Now produce explanations for each answer.

Now final JSON.

--- PROMPT ---

# JSON-Structured Prompt for Calculus Questions

**Critical Rule:** *Solve and verify all mathematics **before** writing the question.* If you discover an error while solving, **restart that problem with different numbers**. After three failed attempts, abandon the problem and start a new one.

**Target Topic(s):** Continuity
**Target Difficulty:** D2 (Medium) - Requires analysis or multi-step reasoning, 3-5 steps
**Number of Questions:** 5

---
## Reference Examples (JSON format)

Study these examples carefully. Pay special attention to:
- How the `distractors` field shows WHY each wrong answer is tempting
- The `common_errors` that students make
- The relationship between `difficulty` and `cognitive_level`
- The precision and accuracy of the explanations

```json

    {"text": "$f$ is differentiable at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$f$ is not continuous at $x = 2$.", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$\\displaystyle\\lim_{x \\to 2} f(x) \\ne 0$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$x = 2$ is a vertical asymptote of the graph of $f$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "The absolute value function has a corner at $x = 2$. It is continuous but not differentiable at that point because the left and right derivatives differ.",
```
```json
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

---

## Workflow (follow in order)
1. **Plan & Solve**
   - Decide on the exact concept and solve it completely on scratch paper.
   - Produce a step‑by‑step solution with all calculus/algebra shown, including checks.
2. **Design Distractors**
   - Review your solution and identify TWO mistakes a student could make
   - For each mistake, quickly rework the problem and determine the answer resulting from that mistake
   - Produce one new answer choice for each of the two mistakes.
   - Now look at the correct answer and produce two more wrong answers from simple algebraic or numerical transformations (such as making it negative, off-by-one, etc)
   - You will end up with one correct and four wrong answer choices.
3. **Write Output**
  - Return a **single JSON array** of question objects.
  - Output JSON only. Do not include any text before or after the array.
   - **Formatting Rules**
     * Use ONLY `$...$` for inline math and `$$...$$` for display math.
     * No HTML tags.

*** User instructions


**Output Format (JSON Array):**

Return your response as a JSON array containing 5 question object(s). Each question should follow this structure:

```json
[
  {
    "text": "Question text here with markdown formatting like **bold** or `code`. Can include code blocks:\n\n```java\nSystem.out.println(\"Hello\");\n```",
    "explanation": "Step-by-step walkthrough of how to arrive at the correct answer. Verify correct answer. Design distractors.",
    "distractors": "Analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and what misconception leads to it",
    "answers": [
      {"text": "Answer text (use markdown like `42` for code)", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Why this is wrong"},
      {"text": "Another answer", "is_correct": true, "explanation": "Why this is correct"},
      {"text": "Third answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Common misconception"},
      {"text": "Fourth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Off-by-one error"},
      {"text": "Fifth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Calculation mistake"}
    ]
  }
]
```

We need to do planning and solving internally. Let's craft 5 continuity problems.

Problem types:

1. Determine continuity of piecewise function at a point; find parameter that makes it continuous.

2. Limit existence based on left/right definition; evaluate continuity.

3. Continuity of rational function with holes; identify domain.

4. Continuity of composition: given f continuous, g defined, etc.

Let's solve each.

Question 1: Piecewise function with parameter a. Example: f(x) = { x^2 + 3x for x ≤ 2, ax + 7 for x > 2 }. Find a that makes f continuous at x=2.

Solution: Evaluate left limit at 2: 2^2 + 3*2 = 4 + 6 = 10. Right-hand value at 2: need to equal 10. Since for x>2, at x=2 we use limit a*2+7 = 2a+7. Set 2a+7 = 10 → 2a = 3 → a = 3/2.

Thus correct answer a = 3/2.

Distractors: (i) solve incorrectly forgetting +7, giving a=5 (since 2a=10). (ii) solve using derivative? maybe a=1.5 vs 2 etc.

Create answer choices: 3/2 (correct), 5/2, 3/4, 2, 5.

Now need to generate explanations.

Question 2: Determine if function is continuous at x= -1: f(x) = (x^2 -1)/(x+1). Simplify: numerator factor (x-1)(x+1). Cancel (x+1) except at x = -1 where original denominator zero. Define f(-1)=? Not given, maybe function defined as piecewise: f(x) = (x^2 -1)/(x+1) for x≠-1, and f(-1)=k (some value). Ask what value of k makes f continuous at -1.

Let's choose that.

Simplify limit as x→-1: after cancel, f(x) = x-1 for x≠-1, limit = (-1)-1 = -2. So need k = -2.

Thus correct answer -2.

Distractors: maybe evaluate original expression plugging -1 gives 0/0 incorrectly leading to answer 0. Or think limit is -1? Or think -1? Provide choices: -2 (correct), -1, 0, 2, 1.

Explain.

Question 3: Identify intervals where function is continuous: f(x) = sqrt(x-1) / (x^2 -4). Determine domain and continuity.

Domain: sqrt requires x-1 ≥ 0 ⇒ x ≥ 1. Denominator cannot be zero: x^2-4 ≠ 0 ⇒ x ≠ ±2. Since domain x≥1, exclude x=2. So domain: [1,2) ∪ (2, ∞). On its domain, numerator and denominator are continuous (except denominator zero). Hence f is continuous on each interval in domain.

Question could ask: The function f is continuous on which of the following intervals?

Provide options:

A) [1,2) only (incorrect, missing (2,∞))

B) (2,∞) only (incorrect, missing first part)

C) [1,2) ∪ (2,∞) (correct)

D) (1,2) ∪ (2,∞) (incorrect, missing endpoint 1)

E) [1,∞) (incorrect includes discontinuity at 2)

Need to explain.

Question 4: Composition continuity: Suppose f is continuous at x=3 and g is continuous at y=f(3). Which of the following must be true about h(x)=g(f(x)) at x=3?

Answer: h is continuous at x=3. That's true.

Distractors: h differentiable (not guaranteed), limit exists but not equal (wrong), h not continuous (false), h has vertical asymptote (false).

Thus correct answer: h is continuous at x=3.

Provide answer choices accordingly.

Question 5: Apply Intermediate Value Theorem: Let f(x) = x^3 - 6x + 2. Show that there is at least one root in interval [1,2]. Which statement justifies it?

We compute f(1) = 1 -6 +2 = -3. f(2) = 8 -12 +2 = -2. Actually both negative, no sign change. Need interval with sign change. Let's compute f(0)=2, f(1)=-3, sign change, root in (0,1). Or maybe choose interval [2,3]: f(2) = -2, f(3) = 27 -18 +2 = 11, sign change, root in (2,3). Use that.

So question: Which of the following statements correctly applies IVT to guarantee a root of f(x)=x^3-6x+2 in interval [2,3]?

Answer choices:

A) f is continuous on [2,3] and f(2)·f(3)<0, so there exists c∈(2,3) with f(c)=0. (correct)

B) f is differentiable on [2,3] and f'(c)=0 (incorrect, that's Rolle's theorem).

C) Since f(2)>0 and f(3)>0, there must be a root (wrong sign).

D) The function has a vertical asymptote in (2,3) (false).

E) The mean value theorem guarantees a root (false).

Thus correct answer A.

--- PROMPT ---

# JSON-Structured Prompt for Calculus Questions

**Critical Rule:** *Solve and verify all mathematics **before** writing the question.* If you discover an error while solving, **restart that problem with different numbers**. After three failed attempts, abandon the problem and start a new one.

**Target Topic(s):** Continuity
**Target Difficulty:** D2 (Medium) - Requires analysis or multi-step reasoning, 3-5 steps
**Number of Questions:** 8

---
## Reference Examples (JSON format)

Study these examples carefully. Pay special attention to:
- How the `distractors` field shows WHY each wrong answer is tempting
- The `common_errors` that students make
- The relationship between `difficulty` and `cognitive_level`
- The precision and accuracy of the explanations

### Example 1
```json
{
  "text": "Let $f$ be the function defined by $f(x) = |x - 2|$ for all $x$. Which of the following statements is true?",

    {"text": "$\\displaystyle\\lim_{x \\to 2} f(x) \\ne 0$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$x = 2$ is a vertical asymptote of the graph of $f$.", "is_correct": false}
  ],
  "explanation": "The absolute value function has a corner at $x = 2$. It is continuous but not differentiable at that point because the left and right derivatives differ.",
```
```json
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

---

## Workflow (follow in order)
1. **Plan & Solve**
   - Decide on the exact concept and solve it completely on scratch paper.
   - Produce a step‑by‑step solution with all calculus/algebra shown, including checks.
2. **Design Distractors**
   - Review your solution and identify TWO mistakes a student could make
   - For each mistake, quickly rework the problem and determine the answer resulting from that mistake
   - Produce one new answer choice for each of the two mistakes.
   - Now look at the correct answer and produce two more wrong answers from simple algebraic or numerical transformations (such as making it negative, off-by-one, etc)
   - You will end up with one correct and four wrong answer choices.
3. **Write Output**
  - Return a **single JSON array** of question objects.
  - Output JSON only. Do not include any text before or after the array.
   - **Formatting Rules**
     * Use ONLY `$...$` for inline math and `$$...$$` for display math.
     * No HTML tags.

*** User instructions


**Output Format (JSON Array):**

Return your response as a JSON array containing 8 question object(s). Each question should follow this structure:

```json
[
  {
    "text": "Question text here with markdown formatting like **bold** or `code`. Can include code blocks:\n\n```java\nSystem.out.println(\"Hello\");\n```",
    "explanation": "Step-by-step walkthrough of how to arrive at the correct answer. Verify correct answer. Design distractors.",
    "distractors": "Analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and what misconception leads to it",
    "answers": [
      {"text": "Answer text (use markdown like `42` for code)", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Why this is wrong"},
      {"text": "Another answer", "is_correct": true, "explanation": "Why this is correct"},
      {"text": "Third answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Common misconception"},
      {"text": "Fourth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Off-by-one error"},
      {"text": "Fifth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Calculation mistake"}
    ]
  }
]
```

We need to produce 8 questions on continuity, medium difficulty, each multiple choice with 5 options, one correct. Must include explanation, distractors field describing why each wrong answer is tempting. Also each answer choice includes its own explanation field.

We must first solve each problem, ensure correct answer. If error discovered, restart with new numbers. Let's design eight problems.

We need to plan each.

Problem 1: continuity of rational function with hole.

Define f(x) = (x^2 - 4)/(x-2) for x ≠ 2, and f(2)=5. Ask: Is f continuous at x=2? Options.

Solve: Simplify numerator: (x-2)(x+2)/(x-2)= x+2 for x≠2. Limit as x→2 is 4. f(2)=5, so not continuous. Answer: "No, because f(2) ≠ limit". Provide distractors like think limit =5; think continuity holds because simplification; think vertical asymptote.

Ok.

Problem 2: piecewise defined function with absolute value, continuity at point.

Define f(x) = { x^2 for x ≤1; 2x-1 for x>1 }. Ask if continuous at x=1. Compute left limit: 1^2 =1. Right limit: 2(1)-1=1. f(1)=1 (since ≤1). So continuous. Options with false statements.

Ok.

Problem 3: continuity of piecewise with sqrt.

f(x) = { sqrt(x) for x ≥0; -sqrt(-x) for x<0 }. Ask continuity at x=0. Both sides approach 0. f(0)=0. So continuous.

Problem 4: continuity of trig function with denominator.

f(x) = sin x / x for x≠0, and f(0)=1. Ask continuity at 0. Limit using known limit =1, matches f(0). So continuous.

Problem 5: continuity of function with floor.

f(x)= floor(x) + {x} where {x}= fractional part. Actually floor+fractional = x for all x? floor(x)+{x}=x. So continuous everywhere. Could ask about point x=2.5.

But may be too easy. Another: define g(x)= {x} (fractional part) at integer points: limit from left = 1-? Actually {x} approaches 1 from left, from right 0, so discontinuous at integers. Ask continuity at x=3.

Problem 6: define h(x)= (x^3-8)/(x-2) for x≠2, h(2)=? Provide value that makes continuous. Determine appropriate value: Simplify to x^2+2x+4, limit at 2 = 4+4+4=12? Wait compute: (x^3-8) = (x-2)(x^2+2x+4). So limit = 2^2+2*2+4 =4+4+4=12. So define h(2)=12. Ask which value must be assigned for continuity. Provide options.

Problem 7: continuity of composite function: f(x)=ln(x^2-4). Domain: x^2-4>0 => |x|>2. Ask whether f is continuous on its domain. Option: Yes; because composition of continuous functions on domain. Distractors: think discontinuous at x=2; think need to check left/right etc.

Problem 8: Weierstrass function? Might be too advanced. Simpler: function defined as f(x)= (x-1) * sin(1/(x-1)) for x≠1, and f(1)=0. Determine continuity at x=1. Since (x-1) factor drives to 0, limit 0, matches, so continuous. Distractors: think limit does not exist due to oscillation.

We also need "distractors" field: analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and what misconception leads to it. Probably summarizing for the question.

Better: Provide code that implements merge sort partially; ask about the content of a temporary array after first merge call.

But need medium difficulty multi-step reasoning.

Let's design: Given an integer array `int[] data = {12, 5, 8, 3, 15, 7};` and a method `partialBubbleSort(int[] arr, int passes)` that performs exactly `passes` passes of the outer loop of bubble sort (i.e., each pass bubbles the largest remaining element to its correct position). The method is called with `passes = 2`. Ask what is the resulting array after the method call.

Students may think full sort or think wrong passes.

Implement code:

```java
public static void partialBubbleSort(int[] a, int passes) {
    int n = a.length;
    for (int p = 0; p < passes; p++) {
        for (int i = 0; i < n - p - 1; i++) {
            if (a[i] > a[i+1]) {
                int temp = a[i];
                a[i] = a[i+1];
                a[i+1] = temp;
            }
        }
    }
}
```

Now compute after 2 passes.

Initial: [12,5,8,3,15,7]

Pass 0 (first pass, p=0, i from 0 to 4):
i0: compare 12>5 true -> swap => [5,12,8,3,15,7]
i1: compare 12>8 true -> swap => [5,8,12,3,15,7]
i2: compare 12>3 true -> swap => [5,8,3,12,15,7]
i3: compare 12>15 false
i4: compare 15>7 true -> swap => [5,8,3,12,7,15]

After first pass: largest 15 at end.

Pass 1 (second pass, p=1, i from 0 to n-p-2 = 6-1-2=3? Wait n=6, p=1, loop i < n-p-1 => i < 6-1-1 =4, so i=0..3)
i0: compare 5>8 false
i1: compare 8>3 true -> swap => [5,3,8,12,7,15]
i2: compare 8>12 false
i3: compare 12>7 true -> swap => [5,3,8,7,12,15]

After second pass, array = [5,3,8,7,12,15].

Thus correct answer.

Now design distractors:

Common misconceptions:
1. Assuming full sort (i.e., complete bubble sort) -> final sorted array ascending: [3,5,7,8,12,15].
2. Off-by-one error in inner loop bound: perhaps they think inner loop runs to `n-p` instead of `n-p-1`, which would cause extra comparison leading to maybe moving 15 earlier? Let's simulate: if inner loop goes i < n-p (i.e., up to index 4 inclusive for p=0, and up to 5 for p=1) So extra compare at end: For p=0, i goes 0..5? Actually n=6, p=0 => i < 6 => i 0..5. Compare a[5] with a[6] out of bounds, unrealistic. But they may think loop runs to n-p-1 inclusive, meaning last index compares a[4] with a[5] same as correct. So not good.

Better distractor: Mistake of not reducing inner loop range after each pass (i.e., using `i < n-1` each pass). Simulate: Two passes but inner loop always goes 0..4 (since n-1=5, i<5 => 0..4). Let's simulate with that erroneous code:

First pass same as correct: after first pass = [5,8,3,12,7,15] (as earlier). Second pass, inner loop 0..4 again:
i0: 5>8 false
i1: 8>3 true => [5,3,8,12,7,15]
i2: 8>12 false
i3: 12>7 true => [5,3,8,7,12,15]
i4: 12>15 false

Result same as correct in this case! Because the extra compare at i=4 with 12 and 15 doesn't swap. So not distinct.

Let's think of other error: They might think bubble sort moves smallest element to front instead of largest to end (i.e., reversed direction). If they mistakenly think passes bubble smallest to front, after two passes the array would be?

Simulate "reverse bubble" where they swap when a[i] < a[i+1] (i.e., they move smallest to end?). Actually to bubble smallest to front they'd iterate from end to start or compare opposite. But typical misconception: After each pass, the smallest ends up at the front. That would be if they incorrectly iterate from right to left. Let's simulate a mistaken version: inner loop same order but condition `if (a[i] < a[i+1]) swap`, which would push larger values left. Let's simulate that:

Start: [12,5,8,3,15,7]

Pass1:
i0: 12<5 false
i1:5<8 true swap => [12,8,5,3,15,7]
i2:5<3 false
i3:3<15 true swap => [12,8,5,15,3,7]
i4:3<7 true swap => [12,8,5,15,7,3]

Result after pass1: [12,8,5,15,7,3]

Pass2:
i0:12<8 false
i1:8<5 false
i2:5<15 true swap => [12,8,15,5,7,3]
i3:5<7 true swap => [12,8,15,7,5,3]
i4:5<3 false

Result after 2 passes: [12,8,15,7,5,3] (not typical). Not a plausible distractor maybe.

Better distractor: They think after two passes, the two largest elements (15 and 12) are at the end in sorted order, leaving rest unchanged from original relative order except they might think after pass2, the second largest (12) also placed before 15. Wait we already have that: after correct algorithm, after two passes, we have [5,3,8,7,12,15] where 12 is second largest placed at index 4. That's correct. So a wrong answer might think the unsorted part retains original order (i.e., after two passes, only the largest two are moved to end, rest unchanged order of original). Original order of the other elements (excluding 12 and 15) is: original array without 12 15: [5,8,3,7] (since 12 at index0, 15 index4). But after moving, they might think result is [5,8,3,7,12,15]. That could be a plausible misconception: they think only biggest two are "bubbled" to end; they ignore that within first pass some other swaps happen that also affect order of other elements (e.g., 8 and 3 may have swapped). Indeed correct result after first pass was [5,8,3,12,7,15]; after second pass [5,3,8,7,12,15]. So they might think after second pass it's [5,8,3,7,12,15] (i.e., second pass only swaps 12 with 7 but not 8 and 3). That could be a distractor.

Let's compute that distractor: [5,8,3,7,12,15] - yep.

Another distractor: Off-by-one pass error: They think passes count includes pass index starting at 1; maybe they think after two passes, they performed 3 passes? Not typical.

Alternatively, they might think inner loop goes to n-p (inclusive) causing one extra comparison that swaps 12 and 7 earlier resulting perhaps array [5,3,7,8,12,15] after two passes? Let's test a variation where second pass inner loop goes i< n-p (i<5) for p=1, so i=0..4 => actually same as correct (since n-p-1 = 4). So no diff.

Maybe they think bubble sort uses a while loop that stops when no swaps happen, but after two passes there may have been swap flag false leading to early termination incorrectly? Hard.

Simplify: Provide five answer choices:
A. `[5,3,8,7,12,15]` – correct.
B. `[3,5,7,8,12,15]` – fully sorted (mistake of assuming full sort).
C. `[5,8,3,7,12,15]` – assumes only largest two moved, rest unchanged (partial but wrong).
D. `[5,3,7,8,12,15]` – swapped 8 and 7 incorrectly (maybe mis-ordered due to misunderstanding of inner loop direction).
E. `[12,5,8,3,7,15]` – only swapped 15 with 7 (think only last element moved each pass incorrectly).

Now verify each wrong answer is plausible.

We'll run limitedBubble(data, 3) maybe.

We must compute result after 3 passes.

Let's compute.

Original: [7,3,5,2,8,1,4] length 7.

Algorithm: outer loop p from 0 to passes-1 inclusive.

Inside inner loop i from 0 to n-p-2 (since condition i < n - p - 1). So each pass reduces inner loop bound by p.

Pass0 (p=0, i 0..5) (n=7)

Start: [7,3,5,2,8,1,4]

i0: compare 7>3 swap => [3,7,5,2,8,1,4]
i1: 7>5 swap => [3,5,7,2,8,1,4]
i2: 7>2 swap => [3,5,2,7,8,1,4]
i3: 7>8? no
i4: 8>1 swap => [3,5,2,7,1,8,4]
i5: 8>4 swap => [3,5,2,7,1,4,8]

End pass0 array: [3,5,2,7,1,4,8] (largest 8 now at end)

Pass1 (p=1, i 0..4) (since n - p -1 = 5, i<5 so i 0-4)

Start: [3,5,2,7,1,4,8]

i0: 3>5? no
i1: 5>2 swap => [3,2,5,7,1,4,8]
i2: 5>7? no
i3: 7>1 swap => [3,2,5,1,7,4,8]
i4: 7>4 swap => [3,2,5,1,4,7,8]

End pass1: [3,2,5,1,4,7,8] (7 now second from end)

Pass2 (p=2, i 0..3) (n-p-1 = 4)

Start: [3,2,5,1,4,7,8]

i0: 3>2 swap => [2,3,5,1,4,7,8]
i1: 3>5? no
i2: 5>1 swap => [2,3,1,5,4,7,8]
i3: 5>4 swap => [2,3,1,4,5,7,8]

End pass2: [2,3,1,4,5,7,8]

Pass3 (p=3, i 0..2) (n-p-1=3)

Start: [2,3,1,4,5,7,8]

i0: 2>3? no
i1: 3>1 swap => [2,1,3,4,5,7,8]
i2: 3>4? no

End pass3: [2,1,3,4,5,7,8]

If we do passes=4 (0,1,2,3) we get result [2,1,3,4,5,7,8].

But we might want passes=3 (0,1,2) leading to [2,3,1,4,5,7,8].

Let's choose passes=3 to have interesting array.

So method limitedBubble(data, 3);

Result: [2,3,1,4,5,7,8].

Now craft answer choices.

Potential distractors:

- Full bubble sorted: [1,2,3,4,5,7,8] (complete sort)
- Missing some swaps: maybe [3,2,1,4,5,7,8] (if first pass omitted)
- Another: [2,1,3,4,5,7,8] (if include extra swap that occurs in pass 3 but not performed)

    "distractors": "Common misconceptions that lead to the wrong choices:\n\n- **B** – Off‑by‑one error in the inner loop bound. Some students think “three passes” means the outer loop has run **twice** (i = 0 and i = 1).       {

      },

# JSON-Structured Prompt for Calculus Questions

**Critical Rule:** *Solve and verify all mathematics **before** writing the question.* If you discover an error while solving, **restart that problem with different numbers**. After three failed attempts, abandon the problem and start a new one.

**Target Topic(s):** Taylor Polynomial Approximations
**Target Difficulty:** D2 (Medium) - Requires analysis or multi-step reasoning, 3-5 steps
**Number of Questions:** 1

---
## Reference Examples (JSON format)

- How the `distractors` field shows WHY each wrong answer is tempting
- The `common_errors` that students make
- The relationship between `difficulty` and `cognitive_level`
- The precision and accuracy of the explanations

---

## Workflow (follow in order)
1. **Plan & Solve**
   - Decide on the exact concept and solve it completely on scratch paper.
   - Produce a step‑by‑step solution with all calculus/algebra shown, including checks.
2. **Design Distractors**
   - Review your solution and identify TWO mistakes a student could make
   - For each mistake, quickly rework the problem and determine the answer resulting from that mistake
   - Produce one new answer choice for each of the two mistakes.
   - Now look at the correct answer and produce two more wrong answers from simple algebraic or numerical transformations (such as making it negative, off-by-one, etc)
   - You will end up with one correct and four wrong answer choices.
3. **Write Output**
  - Return a **single JSON array** of question objects.
  - Output JSON only. Do not include any text before or after the array.
   - **Formatting Rules**
     * Use ONLY `$...$` for inline math and `$$...$$` for display math.
     * No HTML tags.

*** User instructions


**Output Format (JSON Array):**

Return your response as a JSON array containing 1 question object(s). Each question should follow this structure:

```json
[
  {
    "text": "Question text here with markdown formatting like **bold** or `code`. Can include code blocks:\n\n```java\nSystem.out.println(\"Hello\");\n```",
    "explanation": "Step-by-step walkthrough of how to arrive at the correct answer. Verify correct answer. Design distractors.",
    "distractors": "Analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and what misconception leads to it",
    "answers": [
      {"text": "Answer text (use markdown like `42` for code)", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Why this is wrong"},
      {"text": "Another answer", "is_correct": true, "explanation": "Why this is correct"},
      {"text": "Third answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Common misconception"},
      {"text": "Fourth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Off-by-one error"},
      {"text": "Fifth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Calculation mistake"}
    ]
  }
]
```

--- RESPONSE ---

We need to produce one question about Power Series, difficulty Easy (D1). Need to follow critical rule: solve and verify all mathematics before writing the question. If error, restart with different numbers. After three failed attempts, abandon and start new question.

We need to create a JSON array with one question object. Must include fields text, explanation, distractors, answers (each with text, is_correct, explanation). Provide step-by-step solution in explanation that verifies correct answer. Also distractors field analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting.

We must design distractor answers: two based on common mistakes, two from simple mis-transformations.

Let's think of a simple problem: find radius of convergence for a power series, maybe something like sum_{n=0}^\infty (2^n x^n)/ (n!). Actually that converges for all x, radius infinite; not interesting. Maybe sum ( (x-1)^n ) / 4^n => geometric series; radius =4.

But we want easy multi-choice typical.

Let's design: Determine the sum of the series at a specific x, using known formula for geometric series.

Example: Series sum_{n=0}^\infty ( -3x )^n / 5^{n}. That's geometric with ratio r = -3x/5. Sum = 1/(1 - r) = 1/(1 + 3x/5) = 5/(5+3x). Then plug x=2 maybe? Then value = 5/(5+6) =5/11.

Make answer options: correct 5/11, distractor from misusing formula (maybe forgetting the sign -> 5/(5-6) = -5). Another from plugging x incorrectly (x=2 incorrectly yields 5/(5+3*2^?), but maybe mis compute 3*2=7? Not. Another off-by-one: using first term a is maybe -3x/5? Actually series is sum_{n=0}^\infty ( -3x/5)^n. That's correct; but a=1. Mistake may think first term is -3x/5 leading to sum = (-3x/5)/(1-(-3x/5)) = (-3x/5)/(1+3x/5) = (-3x)/(5+3x). For x=2 gives -6/11. That's an answer.

Other miscalc: compute denominator as 1 - r instead of 1 - (-3x/5) leading to 1 - (-6/5) = 1+6/5 = 11/5 but they might invert incorrectly? Actually sum = 1/(1-r) = 1/(1 - (-6/5)) = 1/(1+6/5) = 5/11 correct. Mistake: use 1/(1+r) i.e., treat r as positive: sum = 1/(1- (3x/5)) = 1/(1-6/5) = 1/(-1/5) = -5. That's diff.

Other miscalc: compute numerator incorrectly: maybe they think sum = a/(1-r) with a = -3x/5 giving  -6/5 / (1+6/5) = -6/5 *5/11 = -30/55 = -6/11 (same as earlier). Also they might compute numeric value wrong: 5/11 approximated incorrectly as 0.45 vs 0.5. But answer options should be rational.

Let's produce answer list:

1. "$\\frac{5}{11}$" correct.

2. "$-5$" – arises from using formula incorrectly: sum = 1/(1 - r) but treating r as positive.

3. "$-\\frac{6}{11}$" – arises from mistakenly using first term as r instead of 1.

4. "$\\frac{5}{-1}$" maybe unrealistic. Another simple mis: forgetting negative sign: 5/(5-6) = -5 again (duplicate). Maybe produce "$\\frac{5}{-1}$"? Actually 5/(5-6) = -5, same as above, but we have duplicate. Need distinct.

We can do "$\\frac{6}{11}$" – ignoring the negative sign in numerator maybe.

5. "$\\frac{1}{11}$" – off by factor.

But we need 4 wrong. Let's pick:

- "-5" (sign error)
- "-6/11" (first term error)
- "6/11" (sign error different)
- "1/11" (mis compute denominator)

Make sure each wrong has plausible misconception.

Now craft distractors field: an analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting.

Now we must produce explanation step-by-step, verify final answer.

Let's solve:

Series: sum_{n=0}^\infty ( -3x/5)^n . Find value at x = 2.

Solution: Recognize geometric series with a = 1, r = -3x/5 = -6/5. Converges if |r|<1. At x=2, |r| = 6/5 >1, series diverges! Wait! That's a problem: At x=2 the series diverges. So can't ask value. We need to pick x such that |r|<1. Choose x = 1 maybe: r = -3/5 = -0.6, okay.

Then sum = 1/(1 - r) = 1/(1 - (-3/5)) = 1/(1+3/5) = 1/(8/5)=5/8.

Thus correct answer 5/8.

Let's ensure using a=1.

Mistakes: using denominator 1 - r incorrectly as 1 - (3/5) = 2/5 leading to 5/2 = 2.5 = 5/2? Wait compute: 1/(1 - 3/5) = 1/(2/5) = 5/2 = 2.5, but we may express as "5/2". That's a plausible wrong answer.

Another mistake: using first term a = r = -3/5, sum = r/(1 - r) = (-3/5)/(1+3/5) = (-3/5)/(8/5) = -3/8. So answer -3/8.

# JSON-Structured Prompt for Calculus Questions

**Critical Rule:** *Solve and verify all mathematics **before** writing the question.* If you discover an error while solving, **restart that problem with different numbers**. After three failed attempts, abandon the problem and start a new one.

**Target Topic(s):** Derivatives of Trigonometric Functions
**Target Difficulty:** D2 (Medium) - Requires analysis or multi-step reasoning, 3-5 steps
**Number of Questions:** 1

---
## Reference Examples (JSON format)

Study these examples carefully. Pay special attention to:
- How the `distractors` field shows WHY each wrong answer is tempting
- The `common_errors` that students make
- The relationship between `difficulty` and `cognitive_level`
- The precision and accuracy of the explanations

### Example 1
```json
{
  "text": "If $y = \\sin^3 x$, then $\\frac{dy}{dx} =$. ",
  "answers": [
    {"text": "$3\\cos x$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$3\\cos^2 x$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$3\\sin^2 x$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$-3\\sin^2 x \\cos x$", "is_correct": false},
    {"text": "$3\\sin^2 x \\cos x$", "is_correct": true}
  ],
  "explanation": "Using the chain rule: $\\frac{d}{dx}[\\sin^3 x] = 3\\sin^2 x \\cdot \\cos x$. The outer function is $u^3$ with derivative $3u^2$, and the inner function is $\\sin x$ with derivative $\\cos x$.",
  "difficulty": "",
  "cognitive_level": "",
  "skills": [],
    "distractors": ""
}
```

---

## Workflow (follow in order)
1. **Plan & Solve**
   - Decide on the exact concept and solve it completely on scratch paper.
   - Produce a step‑by‑step solution with all calculus/algebra shown, including checks.
2. **Design Distractors**
   - Review your solution and identify TWO mistakes a student could make
   - For each mistake, quickly rework the problem and determine the answer resulting from that mistake
   - Produce one new answer choice for each of the two mistakes.
   - Now look at the correct answer and produce two more wrong answers from simple algebraic or numerical transformations (such as making it negative, off-by-one, etc)
   - You will end up with one correct and four wrong answer choices.
3. **Write Output**
  - Return a **single JSON array** of question objects.
  - Output JSON only. Do not include any text before or after the array.
   - **Formatting Rules**
     * Use ONLY `$...$` for inline math and `$$...$$` for display math.
     * No HTML tags.

*** User instructions


**Output Format (JSON Array):**

Return your response as a JSON array containing 1 question object(s). Each question should follow this structure:

```json
[
  {
    "text": "Question text here with markdown formatting like **bold** or `code`. Can include code blocks:\n\n```java\nSystem.out.println(\"Hello\");\n```",
    "explanation": "Step-by-step walkthrough of how to arrive at the correct answer. Verify correct answer. Design distractors.",
    "distractors": "Analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting and what misconception leads to it",
    "answers": [
      {"text": "Answer text (use markdown like `42` for code)", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Why this is wrong"},
      {"text": "Another answer", "is_correct": true, "explanation": "Why this is correct"},
      {"text": "Third answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Common misconception"},
      {"text": "Fourth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Off-by-one error"},
      {"text": "Fifth answer", "is_correct": false, "explanation": "Calculation mistake"}
    ]
  }
]
```
*** User instructions

- How the `distractors` field shows WHY each wrong answer is tempting

We need to produce a JSON array with one question object about definition of derivative, medium difficulty (2-5 steps). Must first solve and verify etc. We'll do that.

Let's design a problem: Given a piecewise function, ask for derivative at a point using definition. For instance:

\(f(x) = \begin{cases} x^2+1 & x \ge 1 \\ 3x - 2 & x < 1 \end{cases}\). Find f'(1) using definition.

We need to compute left and right limits. Right derivative: limit as h→0+ of [f(1+h)-f(1)]/h. f(1) from first piece: 1^2+1 = 2. For h>0 small, use first piece: ( (1+h)^2+1 - 2)/h = ((1+2h+h^2+1)-2)/h = (2h + h^2)/h = 2 + h → 2.

Left derivative: h→0- using second piece: f(1+h)=3(1+h)-2 = 3+3h-2 = 1+3h. Then (1+3h - 2)/h = ( -1 + 3h)/h = -1/h + 3. As h→0- (negative small), -1/h → +∞? Wait: -1/h with h negative gives positive large. So limit diverges (does not exist). So derivative does not exist. Since right derivative is finite 2, left limit infinite. Therefore f is not differentiable at 1.

Thus correct answer: "f is not differentiable at x=1."

But we need a multiple choice. Provide statements: maybe options: (A) f'(1) = 2; (B) f'(1) does not exist; (C) f'(1) = -1; (D) f'(1) = 0; (E) f'(1) = 3. The correct is B.

Now we need distractors: two mistakes.

Mistake 1: Only compute right-hand derivative, ignore left piece, get derivative = 2 (option A). That's a typical error.

Mistake 2: Compute left-hand limit incorrectly by plugging h=0 directly: get (f(1)-f(1))/0 leading to 0? Not good. Another plausible mistake: Use same expression for both sides, leading to derivative 0 maybe. Or they compute derivative from left using second piece but incorrectly compute limit: ( (3(1+h)-2) -2)/h = (3+3h-2 -2)/h= ( -1 +3h)/h = -1/h +3, they might think limit as h→0 is 3 (ignoring -1/h term). So they'd get 3 (option E). That is plausible.

Add further wrong options: 0 (maybe they think derivative is zero because function looks constant near point). Off-by-one error: Could be -1 (option C). Provide explanation.

Now we must include "explanation": step-by-step walkthrough verifying correct answer, and "distractors" field: analysis of why each wrong answer is tempting.

Also each answer item needs its own "explanation". Provide.

Now verify logic: Did we compute left-hand derivative correctly? Let's re-evaluate: For x<1 piece, f(x) = 3x -2. At x=1, the function value from left limit is 3*1-2=1. However function definition at x=1 is from the first piece (>=1) so f(1)=2. So there is a jump discontinuity of 1 unit. However for derivative we need limit [f(1+h)-f(1)]/h. Using left values, f(1+h) approaches 1 as h→0-, while f(1)=2, so numerator approx -1 + 3h? Wait compute: f(1+h) = 3(1+h)-2 = 3+3h-2 = 1+3h. Then numerator = (1+3h) - 2 = -1 + 3h. Indeed. So limit = (-1)/h + 3. Since -1/h diverges (to +∞ for h<0), limit does not exist (infinite). So derivative does not exist. Good.

Thus answer: not differentiable.

```java
class Employee {
    private String name;
    private int salary;
    public Employee(String n, int s) {
        name = n;
        salary = s;
    }
    public void raise(int amount) {
        salary += amount;
    }
    public int getSalary() {
        return salary;
    }
}
public class Test {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Employee[] staff = new Employee[3];
        staff[0] = new Employee("Alice", 50000);
        staff[1] = new Employee("Bob", 60000);
        staff[2] = new Employee("Carol", 55000);
        giveRaise(staff, 1, 5000);
        System.out.println(staff[1].getSalary());
    }
    public static void giveRaise(Employee[] arr, int idx, int inc) {
        arr[idx].raise(inc);
    }
}
```

Idea: Given an integer array `int[] data = {3, 7, 2, 9, 5, 8, 1, 4};` and code that modifies array via loops, ask final value of element at index.

Let's craft code:

```
int[] data = {3, 7, 2, 9, 5, 8, 1, 4};
int i, temp = 0;
for (i = 0; i < data.length; i += 2) {
    temp += data[i];
}
for (i = data.length - 1; i >= 0; i -= 3) {
    data[i] = temp % 10;
}
```

Question: After execution, what is the value of `data[5]`?

We need to compute.

First loop: i from 0 to length-1 step 2 (i = 0,2,4,6). Sum data[0]=3, data[2]=2, data[4]=5, data[6]=1 => sum = 3+2+5+1 = 11. temp = 11.

Second loop: i starts at data.length-1 = 7, decrement by 3: i = 7,4,1. For each, set data[i] = temp % 10 = 11 % 10 = 1.

Thus data[7]=1, data[4]=1, data[1]=1.

Other positions unchanged.

Original data: index:0=3,1=7,2=2,3=9,4=5,5=8,6=1,7=4

After modifications:
0:3 (unchanged)
1:1 (changed)
2:2
3:9
4:1 (changed)
5:8 (unchanged)
6:1
7:1 (changed)

Thus data[5] = 8.

Correct answer: 8.

Now design distractors.

Common misconceptions:

1) Miscalculate temp sum: maybe include all elements, sum all -> total sum of array = 3+7+2+9+5+8+1+4 = 39. temp would be 39, temp%10 =9. Then data[5] unchanged 8 still, answer remains 8 (same). Not good.

Better: Mistake in first loop: use i++ instead of i+=2, sum all elements => temp = 39, temp%10=9, then data[5] unchanged. So still 8. Not new. Need distractor that changes data[5] if mistakenly think loop modifies index 5.

Another error: Misinterpret temp%10 as temp%data.length (i.e., 11%8=3). Then set those indices to 3. Data[5] unchanged still 8. Not differentiate.

Error: Off-by-one in second loop start: start at length (8) which is out of bounds, but they may think start at length instead of length-1, leading to no iteration? Not.

Another error: think first loop sums even-indexed elements but includes index 5 (odd) incorrectly? That would affect temp? If they sum indices 0,1,2,3,... they'd get 39, temp%10=9, not change data[5]. So answer still 8.

Need distractors with varied values: maybe 1, 9, 3, 8 (correct). Provide four wrong answers.

A) 8 (correct)

Instead maybe they think data[5] becomes 9 (thinking second loop also modifies index5). That could be derived from temp%10=9 and second loop sets data[5] to 9 (if they think loop hits 5). Let's compute: If they think decrement by 2 (i -=2) from 7: i=7,5,3,1. Then data[5] set to 9. So answer 9.

D) 3 (if they think temp = sum of even-indexed elements =11, but they think temp%10 = 1, then they think data[5] gets 1? Not 3. Another miscalc: maybe they think temp = 11, then assign data[5] = temp (11) truncated or something? Not.

Better produce answer 3 due to confusion with modulo with length: temp % data.length = 11%8 =3, and they think data[5] becomes 3 (assuming second loop hits index5). So answer 3.

E) 0 (if they think temp becomes 0 because they reinitialize temp inside loop incorrectly). Maybe they'd think temp =0 after loops. Then data[5] unchanged 8 -> but they'd answer 0 maybe because they think data[5] set to temp%10 =0? Not realistic.

Let's think of plausible wrong values:

- 1: from second loop modifying index5 (as described) with temp%10=1.

- 9: from second loop modifies index5 with temp%10=9 (if they sum all elements -> 39%10=9)

- 3: from modulo with length 8 -> 11%8=3 and second loop hits index5.

- 6: maybe they think first loop increments i by 1 but also condition i< data.length/2, sum half? Not.

Better to have distinct values.

So final answer set:

1) `8` (correct)

2) `1` (off-by-one in second loop step size such that index5 gets updated with 1)

4) `3` (confusing modulo with array length, also thinks index5 updated)

5) `0` (thinking temp reset to 0 before second loop, thus data[5] stays 0 after assignment). Might be okay.

We'll design new code similar but distinct.

Let's craft code:

```
int[] numbers = {4, 6, 3, 7, 2, 9, 5, 8, 1};
int sum = 0;
int j;

// First loop – add values at odd indices
for (j = 1; j < numbers.length; j += 2) {
    sum += numbers[j];
}

// Second loop – replace every 4th element from the end with the tens digit of sum
for (j = numbers.length - 1; j >= 0; j -= 4) {
    numbers[j] = (sum / 10) % 10;
}
```

Goal: What is value of numbers[4] after execution?

Let's compute.

Array indexes 0..8.

First loop sum odd indices (1,3,5,7)

numbers[1]=6 -> sum=6
numbers[3]=7 -> sum=13
numbers[5]=9 -> sum=22
numbers[7]=8 -> sum=30

So sum=30.

Second loop: start at last index (8) then subtract 4 each iteration: indices visited: 8,4,0.

Compute tens digit of sum: sum=30. sum /10 = 3, %10 => 3. So assign 3 to those positions.

Thus numbers[8]=3, numbers[4]=3, numbers[0]=3.

Now value at index 4 becomes 3.

Hence correct answer is 3.

Now design distractors from misconceptions:

1. Off-by-one / mistake step size: think second loop steps by 3 instead of 4, thus visits indices 8,5,2? Wait start 8, -3 => 5,2,-1. That would modify index 5 not 4. So they would think numbers[4] unchanged (original numbers[4]=2). That would produce answer 2. But we need a distractor from that. So answer 2 (original value) from mistaken loop step.

2. Mistaken sum of all elements instead of odd indices. Sum of all numbers: 4+6+3+7+2+9+5+8+1 = 45. Tens digit = (45/10)%10 = 4. Then second loop visited indices 8,4,0 as correct (since step 4). So numbers[4] becomes 4. Answer 4.

3. Using modulo 10 instead of tens digit (i.e., sum % 10) = 30 %10 =0. Then assign 0 to indices 8,4,0. So answer 0.

4. Assuming sum resets to 0 before second loop, thus assigning 0 to visited indices. Again 0 (but we already have 0). Need distinct distractor: maybe think they use sum/10 without %10, giving 3 (which is correct). Not good. Another possible mistake: they think second loop writes sum itself (30) into those positions, but array of int can hold 30, so numbers[4]=30. That is distinct and plausible.

Thus four distractors: 2, 4, 0, 30.

Provide distractors explanations covering misconceptions.

We need to go through planning then output.

Step 1: Concept: array rotation, integer operations, method side effects, loops. Provide similar but new code.

Let's craft program:

public class RotateCalc {
    public static void rotateRight(int[] a) {
        int tmp = a[a.length - 1];
        for (int i = a.length - 1; i > 0; i--) {
            a[i] = a[i - 1];
        }
        a[0] = tmp;
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        int[] nums = {3, 6, 9, 12, 15, 18};
        // halve each element using integer division
        for (int i = 0; i < nums.length; i++) {
            nums[i] = nums[i] / 3;
        }
        rotateRight(nums);
        nums[4] = nums[4] * 2;
        rotateRight(nums);
        System.out.println(java.util.Arrays.toString(nums));
    }
}

We need to compute final array.

Step trace:

Initial nums = {3,6,9,12,15,18}

Loop divide by 3 (integer division):

3/3 =1, 6/3=2, 9/3=3, 12/3=4, 15/3=5, 18/3=6

Result: [1,2,3,4,5,6]

First rotateRight:

tmp = last element =6

Loop i from 5 down to 1:

i=5: a[5]=a[4]=5
i=4: a[4]=a[3]=4
i=3: a[3]=a[2]=3
i=2: a[2]=a[1]=2
i=1: a[1]=a[0]=1

Then a[0]=tmp=6

Array after first rotation: [6,1,2,3,4,5]

Now nums[4] = nums[4] * 2; index 4 currently is 4. Multiply by 2 -> 8. So array becomes [6,1,2,3,8,5]

Second rotateRight:

tmp = last element =5

Shift:

i=5: a[5]=a[4]=8
i=4: a[4]=a[3]=3
i=3: a[3]=a[2]=2
i=2: a[2]=a[1]=1
i=1: a[1]=a[0]=6

a[0]=tmp=5

Result: [5,6,1,2,3,8]

Print: [5, 6, 1, 2, 3, 8]

Now generate distractors.

Common misconceptions:

1. Left rotation instead of right rotation: shift left by one position each rotate. Let's compute.

Start after division: [1,2,3,4,5,6]

Left rotate (first): shift left, first element moves to end.

Implementation: store first element, shift left, assign first to last.

If left rotate: after first left rotation: [2,3,4,5,6,1]

Then apply nums[4] = nums[4] * 2; index 4 now is element at position 4 (zero-based) => value 6? Wait after left rotation array: indices:0=2,1=3,2=4,3=5,4=6,5=1. Multiply index4 (6) *2 =12. Array becomes [2,3,4,5,12,1]

Second left rotation: rotate left again: store first (2), shift left: [3,4,5,12,1,2]

Result: [3,4,5,12,1,2] Print.

So distractor 1: [3, 4, 5, 12, 1, 2]

2. Assuming method works on copy (no side effect). Then array remains after division: [1,2,3,4,5,6]. Apply nums[4] = nums[4] *2 => index4 (value 5) =>10. So final array would be unchanged except that multiplication, because rotate methods didn't modify. Final: [1,2,3,4,10,6]. Print that.

Distractor 2: [1, 2, 3, 4, 10, 6]

3. Performing the multiplication before any rotations (mistake about order). That is after division you do nums[4] *2 before first rotate. Let's compute.

After division: [1,2,3,4,5,6]

If multiply before first rotate: nums[4] =5*2=10 => array [1,2,3,4,10,6]

Now first rotate right (as correct). Rotate right:

tmp = last =6

Shift:

i=5: a[5]=a[4]=10
i=4: a[4]=a[3]=4
i=3: a[3]=a[2]=3
i=2: a[2]=a[1]=2
i=1: a[1]=a[0]=1

a[0]=6

Array after first rotate: [6,1,2,3,4,10]

Second rotate right (as correct):

tmp = last =10

Shift:

i=5: a[5]=a[4]=4

Compute correct answer.

Let's design:

int[] a = {3, 6, 9, 12, 15};
int[] b = a.clone(); // copy
int[] c = a; // alias
b[1] = 100; // modifies copy only
c[3] = 200; // modifies original via alias
System.out.println(a[3] + " " + b[1] + " " + c[2]);

Let's compute:

Initially a = [3,6,9,12,15]; b = clone -> [3,6,9,12,15]; c alias a.

b[1]=100 => b becomes [3,100,9,12,15]; a unchanged.

c[3]=200 => c alias a, so a becomes [3,6,9,200,15]; b unaffected.

Now print: a[3]=200 ; b[1]=100 ; c[2]= a[2]=9 (since c same as a). So output "200 100 9". That's correct answer.

Now design distractors.

Potential misconceptions:

1. Believing that clone creates a reference to same array (alias) -> then after b[1]=100 would also affect a, and then after c[3]=200 would also affect b (since all same). Let's compute that wrong answer: If b alias a, after b[1]=100: a[1]=100. Then c[3]=200 modifies same array, now a[3]=200. So final a = [3,100,9,200,15]; b same; c same. Print: a[3]=200 ; b[1]=100 ; c[2]=9 => still same? Wait c[2] = a[2] = 9. Actually same result! So that misconception wouldn't change output. Need a different misconception. Maybe they think that clone is shallow copy, but then later modifications to original affect clone for primitive elements? Actually primitives are copied, so they'd think clone still shares elements (maybe think it's deep copy). But clones share nothing, so still same output. Not good.

Consider misconception: thinking that assignment "c = a;" creates a new array copy (like clone). Then they'd think c is separate copy. So modifications to c[3] don't affect a. Then after steps: b clone remains separate, c separate copy of a (initial). So after b[1]=100 modifies b only, a unchanged; c[3]=200 modifies c only, a unchanged. Final arrays: a: [3,6,9,12,15]; b: [3,100,9,12,15]; c: [3,6,9,200,15]. Print: a[3]=12 ; b[1]=100 ; c[2]=9 => "12 100 9". That's a plausible distractor.

Another misconception: off-by-one indexing, think array indices start at 1. Then b[1]=100 they'd think modifies second element? Actually with 1-index they'd think modifies element 1 (first). But in code values may differ.

Let's compute assuming 1-indexed for both b[1] and c[3] and printing indices similarly but they keep code same. That would mean they think b[1] refers to first element (index 0). So they'd think b[0] is illegal maybe? For this case, they'd think b[1]=100 changes a[0] via alias? Let's compute: Starting arrays same as original, but using 1-index mental model.

First, b=clone (separate). c alias a.

b[1]=100 they think changes first element of b (index 0). So b becomes [100,6,9,12,15]; a unchanged.

c[3]=200 they think changes third element (index 2) of a/c. So a: [3,6,200,12,15]; c same.

Now printing a[3] (they think third element? but code uses index 3; they'd think that's fourth element? In mental model they'd map index 3 to fourth element). Hmm confusion.

Let's compute output they'd predict using correct Java semantics but their mental off-by-one: They think a[3] refers to fourth element (value 12). Actually after c[3] (thought as third), a[2] becomes 200. So a is [3,6,200,12,15]. a[3] actual (fourth) is 12 (they think). b[1] actual (second) is 6 (they think maybe 100?). Wait they thought they changed first element, but they think b[1] is first element. So they'd think b[1] = 100. So they'd claim b[1] = 100. c[2] they think refers to element 2 (third) which is 200? Actually c[2] actual is third element (value 200). They may think that. So predicted output: "12 100 200". Let's compute: a[3] -> 12; b[1] -> 100; c[2] -> 200. So "12 100 200". That could be a distractor.

Another misconception: believing that modifying b after cloning affects a (they think shallow copy shares elements). But for int primitives shallow copy still copies values, but they'd think modifications to b affect a. Let's compute: if b shares same array (incorrect), then after b[1]=100, a[1]=100. Then c[3]=200 modifies a (since c alias a) also affects b. So final a= [3,100,9,200,15]; b same. Print: a[3]=200 ; b[1]=100 ; c[2]=9 . That's same as correct though. So not good.

Maybe misconception about order of printing: they think arguments are printed in order b[1], a[3], c[2] etc. So produce a wrong ordering.

Let's design distractors:

Correct answer: "200 100 9"

Distractor A (option B) - misreading assignment as copy, not alias: "12 100 9"

Distractor B (option C) - off-by-one indexing: "12 100 200"

Distractor C (option D) - misreading println order: expecting b[1], a[3], c[2] => "100 200 9"? Wait compute: if they think order is b[1] then a[3] then c[2], actual values: b[1]=100, a[3]=200, c[2]=9 -> "100 200 9". That could be a distractor.

Distractor D (option E) - assuming clone shares same underlying objects (like reference) but still think print order correct, maybe they'd think a[3] changes to 200, b[1] unchanged (since they think b is separate) but they think c alias cause only c changes? Actually they'd think b is separate but changes to a affect b? Wait they might think that cloning creates a shallow copy, but for primitive arrays it's deep copy, but they'd think it's shallow meaning references to same elements (which are primitives, so same value). So they'd think modifications to a after cloning affect b? Suppose they think after clone, b and a share same underlying array (like alias). Then modifications to a affect b. Steps:

After b = a.clone(); they think b shares array with a.

b[1] = 100 modifies a[1] also => a[1]=100.

c = a; alias.

c[3] = 200 modifies a[3] also (already shared). So final a: [3,100,9,200,15]; b same; c same.

Print: a[3]=200; b[1]=100; c[2]=9 => same as correct. So not mis.

Maybe distractor about thinking b[1] affects a[0] due to confusion about arrays and indices? Not good.

Better to keep distractor list similar to example: each wrong answer's explanation describes misconception.

We need four wrong answers. Provide 5 total options (A-E). Use letters not required but concept.
